elogium grauissimum iam totum medius fidius perscriptum ut aboleret, impensis precibus oraui<del status="unremarkable">t</del>; postremo, ni impetrarem, diuersurum me ab ea comminatus sum: mihi hanc ueniam tribueret, malum filium beneficio uinceret, me inuidia omni liberaret. nec prius destiti quam ita fecit. doleo me huncce scrupulum Aemiliano dempsisse, <pb id="p.109" /> tam inopinatam rem ei indicasse. specta quaeso, Maxime, ut hisce auditis subito obstipuerit, ut oculos ad terram demiserit; enim longe sequius ratus fuerat, nec inmerito: mulierem filii contumeliis infectam, meis officiis deuinctam sciebat. de me quoque fuit quod timeret: quiuis uel aeque ut ego spernens hereditatis tamen uindicari de tam inofficioso priuigno non recusasset. haec praecipue sollicitudo eos ad accusationem mei stimulauit: hereditatem omnem mihi relictam falso ex sua auaritia coniectauere. soluo uos in praeteritum isto metu. namque animum meum neque hereditatis neque ultionis occasio potuit loco demouere. pugnaui cum irata matre pro priuigno malo uitricus, ueluti pater pro optimo filio aduersus nouercam, nec satis fuit, ni bonae uxoris prolixam liberalitatem circa me nimio plus aequo coercerem.</p> <milestone unit="section" n="100" /> <p>Cedo tu testamentum iam inimico filio a matre factum me, quem isti praedonem dicunt, uerba singula cum precibus praeeunte<del status="unremarkable">m</del>. rumpi tabulas istas iube, Maxime: inuenies filium heredem, mihi uero tenue nescio quid honoris gratia legatum, ne, si quid ei humanitus attigisset, nomen maritus in uxoris tabulis non haberem. cape ist<del status="unremarkable">a</del>ut matris tuae testamentum, uere hoc quidem inofficiosum; qui<add>d</add>ni? in quo obsequentissimum maritum exheredauit, inimicissimum filium scribsit heredem, immo enimuero non filium, sed Aemiliani spes et Rufini nuptias, set temulentum illud collegium, parasitos tuos. accipe, inquam, filiorum optime, et positis paulisper epistulis amatoriis matris lege potius testamentum: si quid quasi insana scripsit, hic reperies <pb id="p.110" /> et quidem mox a principio: <q direct="unspecified">Sicinius Pudens filius meus mihi heres esto.</q> fateor, qui ho<add>c</add> legerit insanum putabit. hicine filius heres, qui te in ipso fratris sui funere aduocata perditissimorum iuuenum manu uoluit excludere e domo quam ipsa donaueras, qui te sibi a fratre coheredem relictam grauiter et acerbe tulit, qui confestim te cum tuo luctu et maerore deseruit et ad Rufinum et Aemilianum de sinu tuo aufugit, qui <add>t</add>ibi plurimas postea contumelias dixit coram et adiuuante patruo fecit, qui nomen tuum pro tribunalibus uentilauit, qui pudorem tuum tuismet litteris conatus est publice dedecorare, qui maritu<add>m</add> tuum, quem elegeras, quem, ut ipse obiciebat, efflictim amabas, capitis accusauit? aperi quaeso, bone puer, aperi testamentum: facilius insaniam matris sic probabis.</p> <p>Quid abnuis, quid recusas, postquam sollicitudinem de hereditate materna reppulisti? at ego hasce tabulas, <milestone unit="section" n="101" /> Maxime, hic ibidem pro pedibus tuis abicio testorque me deinceps incuriosius habiturum, quid Pudentilla testamento suo scribat. ipse iam, ut libet, matrem suam de cetero exoret: mihi, ut ultra pro eo deprecer, locum non reliquit. ipse iam, ut <add>qui</add> sui potens ac uir acerbissimas litteras matri dictet, iram eius deleniat; qui potuit perorare, poterit exorare. mihi iam dudum satis est, si non modo crimina obiecta plenissime dilui, uerum etiam radicem iudicii huius, id est hereditatis quaesitae inuidiam, funditus sustuli. <pb id="p.111" /></p> <p>Illud etiam, <del status="unremarkable">c</del> ne quid omnium praeteream, priusquam peroro, falso obiectum reuincam. dixistis me magna pecunia mulieris pulcherrimum praedium meo nomine emisse. dico exiguum herediolum LX milibus nummum, id quoque non me, sed Pudentillam suo nomine emisse, Pudentillae nomen in tabulis esse, Pudentillae nomine pro eo agello tributum dependi. praesens est quaestor publicus, cui depensum est, Coruinius Celer, uir ornatus; adest etiam tutor auctor mulieris, uir grauissimus et sanctissimus, omni cum honore mihi nominandus, Cassius Longinus. quaere, Maxime, cuius emptionis auctor fuerit, quantulo pretio mulier locuples agellum suum praestinarit.—</p> <p><del status="unremarkable">testimonium Cassi Longini tutoris et Coruini Clementis quaestor(is)</del></p> <p>Estne ita ut dixi? uspiam in hac emptione nomen meum ascriptum est? num ipsum heredioli pretium inuidiosum est, num uel hoc saltem in me collatum?</p> <milestone unit="section" n="102" /> <p>Quid etiam est, Aemiliane, quod non te iudice refutauerim? quod pretium magiae meae repperisti? cur ergo Pudentillae animum ueneficiis flecterem? quod ut ex ea commodum caperem? uti dotem mihi modicam potius quam a<del status="unremarkable">m</del>mpla<add>m</add> diceret? o praeclara carmina. an ut eam dotem filiis suis magis restipularetur quam penes me sineret? quid addi ad hanc magiam potest? <pb id="p.112" /> an uti rem familiarem suam meo adhortatu pleramque filiis condonasset, quae nihil illis ante me maritum fuerat largita, mihi <add>nihil</add> quicquam impertiret? o graue ueneficium dicam an ingratum beneficium. an ut testamento, quod irata filio scribebat, filium potius, cui offensa erat, quam me, cui deuincta, heredem relinqueret? hoc quidem multis cantaminibus difficile impetraui. putate uos causam non apud Cl(audium) Maximum agere, uirum aequum et iustitiae pertinacem, sed alium aliquem prauum et saeuum iudicem substituite, accusationum fautorem, cupidum condem<del status="unremarkable">p</del>nandi: date ei quod sequatur, ministrate uel tantulam uerisimilem occasionem secundum uos pronuntiandi; saltim fingite aliquid, eminiscimini quod respondeatis, qui uos ita rogarit. et quoniam omnem conatum necesse est quaepiam causa praecedat, respondete qui Apuleium dicitis animum Pudentillae magicis illectamentis ad<del status="unremarkable">h</del>ortum, quid ex ea petierit, cur fecerit. formam eius uoluerat? negatis. diuitias saltim concupierat? negant tabulae dotis, negant tabulae donationis, negant tabulae testamenti, in quibus non modo non cupide appetisse, uerum etiam dure reppulisse liberalitatem suae uxoris <del status="unremarkable">h</del>ostenditur. quae igitur alia causa est? quid ommutuistis? quid tacetis? ubi illud libelli uestri atrox principium nomine priuigni mei form<del status="unremarkable">orm</del>atum: <q direct="unspecified">hunc ego, domine Maxime, <milestone unit="section" n="103" /> reum apud te facere institui?</q> quin igitur addis; <q direct="unspecified">reum magistrum, reum uitricum, reum deprecatorem?</q> sed quid deinde? <q direct="unspecified">plurimorum maleficiorum et manifestissimorum.</q> <pb id="p.113" /> cedo unum de plurimis, cedo dubium uel saltem obscurum de manifestissimis. ceterum ad haec, quae obiecistis, numera an binis uerbis respondeam. <q direct="unspecified">dentes sp<add>l</add>endidas:</q> ignosce munditiis. <q direct="unspecified">specula inspicis:</q> debet philosophus. <q direct="unspecified">uersus facis:</q> licet fieri. <q direct="unspecified">pisces exploras:</q> Aristoteles docet. <q direct="unspecified">lignum consecras:</q> Plato suadet. <q direct="unspecified">uxorem ducis:</q> leges iubent. <q direct="unspecified">prior natu is<add>ta</add> est:</q> solet fieri. <q direct="unspecified">lucrum sectatus es:</q> dotalis accipe, donationem recordare, testamentum lege. quae si omnia affatim retudi, si calumnias omnes refutaui, si me in omnibus non modo criminibus, uerum etiam maledictis procul a culpa <del status="unremarkable">philosophiae</del> tutus sum, si philosophiae honorem, qui mihi salute mea antiquior est, nusquam minui, immo contra ubique si cum septem pennis eum tenui: si haec, ut dico, ita sunt, possum securus existimationem tuam reuereri quam potestatem uereri, quod minus graue et uerendum mihi arbitror a<del status="unremarkable">c</del> procons(ule) damnari quam si a tam bono tamque emendato uiro improber.</p> <p>Dixi. <pb id="p.114" /> </p></body></text></TEI.2>
ChatGPT
In this passage, Apuleius continues his defense by addressing the various accusations leveled against him, particularly those related to alleged magical practices. He asserts that the claims are baseless and motivated by malice and envy. Apuleius emphasizes that the accusations of sorcery were fabricated by his enemies, who aimed to create discord between him and Pudentilla’s family for their own gain. He points out the absurdity of the charges, questioning the logic and motives behind them. For instance, he challenges the claim that he married Pudentilla for her wealth, explaining that he did not seek to alienate her from her children but rather encouraged reconciliation and peace within the family. Apuleius also explains that he persuaded Pudentilla to donate her property to her sons, which goes against the image of a greedy sorcerer. Moreover, Apuleius refutes the idea that he used magical charms to win Pudentilla’s affection. He argues that if he truly desired wealth, he would have acted differently to secure it. He expresses frustration with the irrational nature of the accusations, pointing out that none of his actions fit the profile of someone who would resort to magic for personal gain. He dismisses the notion that he would employ spells to marry an older woman with moderate wealth, when, in fact, he could have used those same alleged powers to seek out a wealthier or more advantageous match. Apuleius concludes by systematically dismantling the accusations and highlighting the inconsistencies and weaknesses in his accusers' arguments. He reiterates that his actions were motivated by virtue, honor, and respect for philosophy, and that his accusers have failed to prove any wrongdoing. His final remarks underscore his confidence in the strength of his defense and his faith in the judgment of the court, demonstrating his unwavering commitment to truth and integrity.

Here’s an English translation of the final section of Apuleius' *Apology*: --- In this last section, Apuleius responds to accusations of using magic to win Pudentilla's love and wealth, and of sowing discord in her family. He emphasizes the baselessness of the accusations, addressing them one by one. He states that if he had truly been motivated by greed, he would have sought to alienate Pudentilla from her children rather than encourage reconciliation. He describes the lengths he went to in persuading Pudentilla to restore her wealth to her sons, which contradicts the image of a sorcerer exploiting her for gain. Apuleius also tackles accusations of magical practices by explaining the absurdity of these claims. He argues that no reasonable cause for suspicion has been shown, and he asks why he would even attempt to manipulate Pudentilla, a woman of moderate means, if his true aim were wealth. He highlights that Pudentilla's gifts to her children were made under his guidance and that these actions are inconsistent with those of a man driven by greed or malevolence. V. purgem, cui haud pridem tirocinio orationis suae fuerat a me commendatus; quippe compererat ante paucos dies omnia me, ut acta erant, ad eum perscripsisse. id quoque a me impetrat. itaque acceptis litteris Carthaginem pergit, ubi iam prope exacto consulatus sui munere Lollianus Auitus te, Maxime, opperiebatur. <del status="unremarkable">h</del>is epistulis meis lectis pro sua eximia humanitate gratulatus Pontiano, quod cito <del status="unremarkable">h</del>errorem suum correxisset, rescripsit mihi per eum quas litteras, di boni, qua doctrina, quo lepore, qua uerborum amoenitate simul et iucunditate, prorsus ut <q direct="unspecified">uir bonus dicendi peritus.</q> scio te, Maxime, libenter eius litteras auditurum; et quide<add>m</add> si praelegam, mea uoce pronuntiabo. cedo tu Auiti epistulas, ut quae semper ornamento mihi fuerunt sint nunc etiam saluti. at tu licebit aquam sinas fluere; namque optimi uiri litteras ter et quater aueo quantouis temporis dispendio lectitare.— <pb id="p.104" /></p> <milestone unit="section" n="95" /> <p>Non sum nescius debuisse me post istas Auiti litteras perorare. quem enim laudatorem locupletiorem, quem testem uitae meae sanctiorem producam, quem denique aduocatum facundiorem? multos in uita mea Romani nominis disertos uiros sedulo cognoui, sed sum <del status="unremarkable">m</del>aeque neminem ammiratus. nemo est hodie, quantum mea opinio fert, alicuius in eloquentia laudis et spei, quin Auitus esse longe malit, si cu<add>m</add> eo se remota inuidia uelit conferre; quippe omnes fandi uirtutes paene diuersae in illo uiro congruunt. quamcumque ora<add>tio</add>nem struxerit Auitus, ita illa erit undique sui perfecte absoluta, ut in illa neque Cato grauitatem requirat neque Laelius lenitatem nec Gracchus impetum nec Caesar calorem nec <add>H</add>ortensius distributionem nec Caluus argutias nec parsimoniam Salustius nec opulentiam Cicero: prorsus, inquam, ne omnis persequar, si Auitum audias, neque additum quicquam uelis neque detractum neque autem aliquid commutatum.</p> <p>Video, Maxime, quam benigne audias, quae in amico tuo Auito recognosces. tua me comitas, ut uel pauca dicerem de eo, inuitauit. at non usque adeo tuae beniuolentiae indulgebo, ut mihi permittam iam propemodum fesso in causa prorsus ad finem inclinata de egregiis uirtutibus eius nunc demum incipere, quin potius eas integris uiribus et tempori libero seruem. <milestone unit="section" n="96" /> nunc enim mihi, quod aegre fero, a commemoratione tanti uiri ad pestes istas oratio reuoluenda est.</p> <p>Audesne te ergo, Aemiliane, cum Auito conferre? enim Latine loqui neque uult neque potest. audisti, Maxime, paulo ante, pro nefas, priuignum meum, fratrem Pontiani, diserti iuuenis, uix singulas syllabas fringultientem, cum ab eo quaereres, dona<add>s</add>setne illis mater quae ego dicebam me adnitente donata.</p> <milestone unit="section" n="99" /> <p>Testor igitur te, Claudi Maxime, uosque, qui in consilio estis, uosque etiam, qui tribunal mecum adsistitis, haec damna et dedecora morum eius patruo huic et candidato illo socero adsignanda meque posthac boni consulturum, quod talis priuignus curae meae iugum ceruice excusserit, neque postea pro eo matri eius supplicaturum. nam, quod paenissime oblitus sum, nuperrime cum testamentum Pudentilla post mortem Pontiani filii sui in mala ualetudine scrib<del status="unremarkable">s</del>eret, diu sum aduersus illam renisus, ne hunc ob tot insignis contumelias, ob tot iniurias exheredaret; enim longe sequius ratus fuerat, nec inmerito: mulierem filii contumeliis infectam, meis officiis deuinctam sciebat. de me quoque fuit quod timeret: quiuis uel aeque ut ego spernens hereditatis tamen uindicari de tam inofficioso priuigno non recusasset. haec praecipue sollicitudo eos ad accusationem mei stimulauit: hereditatem omnem mihi relictam falso ex sua auaritia coniectauere. soluo uos in praeteritum isto metu. namque animum meum neque hereditatis neque ultionis occasio potuit loco demouere. pugnaui cum irata matre pro priuigno malo uitricus, ueluti pater pro optimo filio aduersus nouercam, nec satis fuit, ni bonae uxoris prolixam liberalitatem circa me nimio plus aequo coercerem.</p> <milestone unit="section" n="100" /> <p>Cedo tu testamentum iam inimico filio a matre factum me, quem isti praedonem dicunt, uerba singula cum precibus praeeunte<del status="unremarkable">m</del>. rumpi tabulas istas iube, Maxime: inuenies filium heredem, mihi uero tenue nescio quid honoris gratia legatum, ne, si quid ei humanitus attigisset, nomen maritus in uxoris tabulis non haberem. cape ist<del status="unremarkable">a</del>ut matris tuae testamentum, uere hoc quidem inofficiosum; qui<add>d</add>ni? in quo obsequentissimum maritum exheredauit, inimicissimum filium scribsit heredem, immo enimuero non filium, sed Aemiliani spes et Rufini nuptias, set temulentum illud collegium, parasitos tuos. accipe, inquam, filiorum optime, et positis paulisper epistulis amatoriis matris lege potius testamentum: si quid quasi insana scripsit, hic reperies <pb id="p.110" /> et quidem mox a principio: <q direct="unspecified">Sicinius Pudens filius meus mihi heres esto.</q> fateor, qui ho<add>c</add> legerit insanum putabit. hicine filius heres, qui te in ipso fratris sui funere aduocata perditissimorum iuuenum manu uoluit excludere e domo quam ipsa donaueras, qui te sibi a fratre coheredem relictam grauiter et acerbe tulit, qui confestim te cum tuo luctu et maerore deseruit et ad Rufinum et Aemilianum de sinu tuo aufugit, qui <add>t</add>ibi plurimas postea contumelias dixit coram et adiuuante patruo fecit, qui nomen tuum pro tribunalibus uentilauit, qui pudorem tuum tuismet litteris conatus est publice dedecorare, qui maritu<add>m</add> tuum, quem elegeras, quem, ut ipse obiciebat, efflictim amabas, capitis accusauit? aperi quaeso, bone puer, aperi testamentum: facilius insaniam matris sic probabis.</p> <p>Quid abnuis, quid recusas, postquam sollicitudinem de hereditate materna reppulisti? at ego hasce tabulas, <milestone unit="section" n="101" /> Maxime, hic ibidem pro pedibus tuis abicio testorque me deinceps incuriosius habiturum, quid Pudentilla testamento suo scribat. ipse iam, ut libet, matrem suam de cetero exoret: mihi, ut ultra pro eo deprecer, locum non reliquit. ipse iam, ut <add>qui</add> sui potens ac uir acerbissimas litteras matri dictet, iram eius deleniat; Here’s the English translation of the Latin text provided: --- Having a fault, the woman who either was so intolerable as to be divorced, or so insolent as to divorce. Because of these things and others, widows, augmented by their dowries, entice suitors. This Pudentilla also would have done with another husband if she had not found a philosopher who scorned dowries. --- Section 93: But come now, if I had desired the woman out of greed, what would have been more useful for me to gain control of her household than to sow discord between the mother and her children, alienating their love for her, so that I could possess the isolated woman more freely and completely? Would this not have been the way of a predator, as you imagine? In reality, I promoted peace, harmony, and piety, not only did I not sow new hatreds, but I also completely eradicated old ones. I advised my wife—whose wealth, as they say, I had already devoured—indeed, I persuaded her to give back to her sons the money they demanded, which I mentioned earlier, without delay, in properties appraised cheaply and at the prices they wanted. Besides this, from her own resources, I urged her to donate the most fertile fields, a grand, richly decorated house, a large supply of wheat, barley, wine, oil, and other products, and even more than 400 slaves, and numerous livestock neither few nor of insignificant value. So that she might both have peace from what she gave them and lure them to the rest of her inheritance. All this I wrested from Pudentilla against her will—for she will allow me to say this as it was—I extorted it with great pleading from one who was reluctant and angry, reconciled the mother to her children, and with this first act of a stepfather, I greatly enriched my stepsons. --- Section 94: This became known throughout the entire city. Everyone cursed Rufinus and praised me. Pontianus, before this donation was completed, came to us with a very different attitude from that of his brother. He threw himself at our feet, begging forgiveness and oblivion of all past matters, tearfully kissing our hands, saying he regretted having listened to Rufinus and others like him. He then pleaded that I excuse him to Lollianus Avitus, a most distinguished man, whom I had recently recommended him to in his first oration. He had learned that, just a few days prior, I had written to him about everything that had happened. He received my letter, went to Carthage, where Lollianus Avitus, now near the end of his consulship, was waiting for you, Maximus. After reading my letters, Lollianus, in his extraordinary humanity, congratulated Pontianus for quickly correcting his error, and through him, he wrote back to me letters filled with wisdom, charm, and delight in both words and spirit, as only a learned man could. I know, Maximus, you would enjoy hearing his letters, and if you permit me, I shall read them aloud with my own voice. Hand over the letters of Avitus, that which has always adorned me may now also serve my defense. Meanwhile, you may let the water flow, for I eagerly wish to read the letters of such an excellent man again and again, no matter how much time it takes. --- Section 95: I am aware that I should have concluded my defense after these letters of Avitus. For what more trustworthy witness or advocate of my life could I produce? I have known many eloquent men of Roman heritage in my life, but I have admired none so much as Avitus. There is no one today, as far as I know, who aspires to fame or hope in eloquence who would not prefer to be Avitus, if envy were removed and one could compare themselves to him. For nearly all virtues of eloquence, diverse as they are, converge in that man. Whatever speech Avitus composes, it is so perfectly complete in all respects that neither Cato would find it lacking in gravity, nor Laelius in gentleness, nor Gracchus in passion, nor Caesar in warmth, nor Hortensius in arrangement, nor Calvus in wit, nor Sallustius in restraint, nor Cicero in richness: indeed, to put it briefly, if you listen to Avitus, you would neither wish anything added, removed, nor even altered. --- I see, Maximus, how kindly you listen to this, recognizing what you know in your friend Avitus. Your graciousness encouraged me to say a few words about him. But I will not indulge too much in your goodwill to permit myself, now almost exhausted and with the case nearly at an end, to begin discussing his exceptional virtues. Rather, I shall reserve them for a time when I am fresh and free from the task at hand. Now, as much as I regret it, I must turn my speech from this great man back to these pests. --- Section 96: Do you dare, Aemilianus, to compare yourself with Avitus? He calls me a good man, whose intellect he praises in his letters, yet you attack me with accusations of sorcery and crime? Or do you feel more anguish over my supposedly invading Pudentilla’s house and plundering her wealth than Pontianus did, who made amends with me before Avitus, thanking me even in his absence? Imagine I read Avitus’s letters rather than those of Pontianus. What charges or accusations could anyone raise in this matter? Pontianus himself regarded the gifts from his mother as a boon from me, rejoicing in me as his stepfather with deep affection. I wish he had returned safely from Carthage. Or, given fate decreed otherwise, I wish, Rufinus, that you had not obstructed his final judgment. What thanks might he have given me, either in person or finally in his will? He wrote to me from Carthage, full of honor and affection. I ask you, Maximus, allow these letters to be read for a while, so that his brother, my accuser, may understand how greatly he falls short in comparison to his brother, a man of the utmost integrity. --- Section 97: Have you heard the names your brother Pontianus gave me—calling me his father, his master, his teacher—even at the end of his life? And your similar letters I would bring forth, were it worth the time. I would rather produce his recent will, incomplete though it is, where he made mention of me most dutifully and honorably. Yet Rufinus did not allow this will to be drawn up or completed, ashamed of the lost inheritance he had once calculated by the price of nightly services when he was Pontianus’s father-in-law for just a few months. Moreover, he consulted some Chaldeans on what profits might come from marrying off his daughter, who, as I hear, foretold that her husband would die within a few months. But the gods willed it otherwise, and like a blind beast, he gnashed his teeth in vain. For Pontianus, far from leaving Rufinus’s daughter as heir, did not even honor her with a respectable legacy, merely bequeathing her some linens, valued at around two hundred denarii, making it clear he did so more out of anger than forgetfulness. He appointed both his mother and his brother as heirs in both this will and the earlier one that was read. --- Section 98: So it is claimed that this girl, through the seductions of a harlot and the inducements of her father, a pimp, has captured and possessed him. Since his brother breathed his last, he abandoned his mother and moved to his uncle, so their plans might proceed unhindered. Indeed, Aemilianus supports Rufinus and hopes for the outcome. He tempers his own expectations in this matter as a good uncle and nurtures him, knowing he will be the legitimate, if not rightful, heir to an intestate son. I wish, by Hercules, this revelation had not come from me; it is not my nature to bring hidden suspicions into the open. But you, who prompted this, acted poorly. Many are wondering, Aemilianus, at your sudden interest in the boy, whom you barely recognized before as your brother's son, so that now you allow him everything without restraint, in a way that makes even the most suspicious minds take note. You received him from us untrained; you promptly outfitted him; when he was with us, he went to tutors. Now, he skips school to indulge in pleasure, avoiding serious friends, consorting with the lowest youths among whores and cups, conducting himself at your household as the master of the family, the leader of gatherings. Even in the gladiatorial school, he is a frequent visitor, learning the names of gladiators, their fights, and wounds, as if a noble boy taught by a lanista. He speaks only Punic, for anything from his mother he speaks Greek, refusing to speak Latin. You heard, Maximus, just now, alas, my stepson, Pontianus’s brother, that articulate youth, barely able to stammer single syllables when you asked whether his mother had given them the gifts I said I helped provide. --- Section 99: I call upon you, Claudius Maximus, and you who sit in counsel, and even those who stand with me at the tribunal, to testify that these damages and disgraces to his character are owed to his uncle and that wretched would-be father-in-law, and that I shall henceforth be grateful to be free of this stepson, who has thrown off the yoke of my care, and I shall no

longer plead with his mother on his behalf. For, as I almost forgot, when Pudentilla, in her poor health, recently drafted her will after the death of her son Pontianus, I struggled against her to prevent her from disinheriting this son for his many remarkable insults and injuries. I pleaded extensively that she erase the harsh condemnation she had written; finally, I threatened to leave her if she would not relent, and urged her to overcome a bad son with kindness and spare me any reproach. I stopped only when she did as I requested. I regret having removed this difficulty for Aemilianus, revealing such an unexpected thing to him. Observe, Maximus, how upon hearing this, he immediately lowered his eyes to the ground; he had expected a far different outcome, and not unjustly so: he knew of the woman’s anguish over her son’s insults and her obligation to me. There was also reason for him to fear what I might do: any man, like myself, despising inheritance, would still seek to avenge such disrespect from an ungrateful stepson. --- Section 100: Here, Aemilianus, hand over that will your mother made in anger towards her son, as they call me a robber. Order these tablets to be torn apart, Maximus: you will find the son named as heir, and me, however, left with a trivial honorarium—just so my name might not be omitted from her will. Take this testament from your mother, indeed as it is most unfitting: in which the most devoted husband was disinherited, while the son, most hostile to her, was written in as heir—not truly a son, but rather Aemilianus’s hopes and Rufinus’s marital aspirations. Take it, most dear son, and set aside your mother’s love letters, read the will instead; you will discover whatever madness she may have written, and indeed right from the beginning: “Sicinius Pudens, my son, shall be my heir.” I admit, whoever reads this would think it mad. Is this the son as heir, who during his brother’s funeral sought to exclude you from the house which she herself had given you, who gravely resented that you were made co-heir with his brother, who abandoned you in your grief, and fled to Rufinus and Aemilianus from your arms, who later spoke countless insults to you in person and with his uncle’s aid, who tried to publicize your shame by your own letters, who accused the husband you had chosen—whom, as he objected, you loved passionately—of a capital crime? Open it, good boy, open the will, and thus more easily prove your mother’s insanity. --- Section 101: Why do you refuse, why do you hesitate, now that you have cast aside your worry over the maternal inheritance? Yet here I lay down these tablets at your feet, Maximus, and I declare that I will care even less in the future about whatever Pudentilla may write in her will. Now he must plead with his mother on his own behalf, for he has left no room for me to intercede any further. As he who is now master of himself, a man who dictates harsh letters to his mother, let him soothe her wrath; if he could argue, he can beg. It is enough for me now if I have not only refuted the accusations against me, but also eradicated the very root of this trial, that is, the malice towards an inheritance sought unjustly. --- Section 102: One last matter remains, lest I leave anything unaddressed before I conclude. You have falsely accused me of purchasing a fine estate with the woman’s wealth in my own name. I say that I bought a small farm for 60,000 sesterces, and that, moreover, it was not I, but Pudentilla, who bought it in her name. The name on the documents is hers; the tribute for that small estate was paid under Pudentilla’s name. Corvinius Celer, the public quaestor, is present, to whom the tribute was paid—a distinguished man. Also present is the woman’s tutor and advisor, Cassius Longinus, a most honorable and revered man. Inquire, Maximus, who was the authority for this purchase and at what small price the wealthy woman bought her own little farm. --- Section 103: Thus I have answered everything, Aemilianus, even by your own standards. What price of my magic did you find? Why would I need to bend Pudentilla’s will with potions? Was it so I could claim a moderate dowry rather than a large one? What excellent incantations! Or was it so that she would transfer her estate more to her sons than to me? What more can be added to this magic? Or so that, through my urging, she should leave most of her estate to her sons, when before me, as her husband, she had given them nothing, giving me nothing at all? O severe witchcraft, shall I call it, or thankless beneficence? Or was it so that in her will, which she wrote in anger towards her son, she would leave the inheritance to him, whom she resented, rather than to me, to whom she was devoted? Indeed, it took many incantations to achieve this. Imagine yourselves not before Claudius Maximus, a man fair and just, but some cruel and corrupt judge, one partial to accusations, eager to condemn. Give him something to follow, provide even the slightest plausible pretext for him to pronounce in your favor. At least invent something, devise something to answer, when he asks you. And since every action must be motivated by some reason, answer why you say that Apuleius beguiled Pudentilla’s mind with magic. What did he seek from her, why did he do it? Did he desire her beauty? You deny it. Did he crave her wealth, at least? The dowry documents, the donation records, the will all deny it, showing not only that he did not eagerly covet but even harshly rejected his wife’s generosity. What then is the cause? Why are you silent? Why do you not speak? Where is that harsh opening of your pamphlet, so shaped by my stepson’s influence: “I intend, Lord Maximus, to bring this man to trial before you.” Why then do you not add: “On charges against a teacher, a stepfather, an advocate?” But what follows? “Of many and most manifest crimes.” Produce one of these many, bring forth even one that is doubtful or obscure among these most manifest. Against all the charges, have I not, in every way, with the very words themselves, cleared myself? --- If there is anything else, Aemilianus, which I have not answered in your judgment, what price of magic have you discovered? Why, then, should I need to bend Pudentilla’s will with poisons? Was it so that I might gain something from her, that she would bequeath a modest dowry to me rather than an ample one? What splendid incantations. Or was it so that she should return that dowry more to her children than leave it with me? What more can be added to this magic? Or was it so that, through my urging, she should give most of her estate to her children, when she had given them nothing before me, her husband, and nothing at all to me? Severe witchcraft indeed, shall I call it, or thankless beneficence. --- The final statements reinforce Apuleius’s assertion that he acted without any selfish motives, countering every accusation and laying bare the baselessness of the charges against him.

This is the Apology of Apuleius. Give me a summary of his defense and what we learn about magic in the ancient roman world from this text.
ChatGPT
In *The Apology of Apuleius*, also known as *Pro se de magia*, Apuleius defends himself against accusations of practicing magic to seduce and marry a wealthy widow, Pudentilla. His accusers, led by Pudentilla's son Sicinius Aemilianus, claim that Apuleius used spells and enchantments to gain control over her wealth. In his defense, Apuleius addresses each charge meticulously, using both logical arguments and rhetorical flair. He argues that his actions were guided by piety and integrity, and that he sought reconciliation between Pudentilla and her sons rather than personal gain. ### Summary of Apuleius’s Defense: 1. Motive: Apuleius questions the motive attributed to him. If he were truly interested in wealth, he asks, why would he encourage Pudentilla to give substantial gifts and properties to her sons? He argues that, if anything, his actions demonstrate selflessness and family loyalty. 2. Lack of Evidence: He refutes each specific accusation as either misinterpreted or unfounded, such as the claim that he purchased an estate in his own name. He presents witnesses, documentation, and even public records to demonstrate that these accusations are based on misunderstandings or fabrications. 3. Philosophical Interests Misinterpreted: Apuleius suggests that his philosophical pursuits were mistaken for magical practices. For example, he explains that his interest in natural science and philosophy (like studying fish or owning mirrors) was taken out of context by those who don’t understand scholarly pursuits, and that activities like dedicating wood to Plato reflect philosophical practices, not sorcery. 4. Reputation and Character Witnesses: He introduces letters from notable people, such as Lollianus Avitus, as character references. He underscores that respectable people view him as a man of honor, not a magician. 5. Absurdity of Claims: Apuleius uses wit and satire to mock the accusations, showing that they lack a rational basis. For instance, he jests that if he really were a sorcerer, he could have enchanted a wealthier or younger woman. He also questions why Pudentilla’s other relationships are not subject to the same suspicion, underscoring that the accusations are more rooted in malice than fact. ### Insights into Magic in Ancient Rome: 1. Legal and Social Perceptions of Magic: The text reveals that magic was considered a serious crime, associated with harm and deceit. Sorcery was feared because it was believed to involve manipulations that could lead to death, illness, or the undermining of social order. Magic was often connected to maleficium (acts of malevolent magic), which could be prosecuted in court. 2. Philosophy vs. Magic: Apuleius illustrates that the boundary between magic and philosophy was often blurred. His accusers equate his intellectual pursuits with magic, showing that in Roman society, any form of esoteric knowledge or practice could be suspect. Even basic medical or scientific knowledge might be misconstrued as sorcery by those unfamiliar with these practices. 3. Role of Wealth and Marriage: The case against Apuleius highlights concerns about wealth, inheritance, and marriage. Magical accusations were sometimes used as a way to contest relationships or marriages that might disrupt inheritance or family wealth distribution. This aspect shows the intersection between legal systems, family dynamics, and accusations of magic in Roman society. 4. Rhetorical Defense against Magic: Apuleius uses classical rhetoric to challenge and dismantle the accusations. By doing so, he demonstrates that reasoned argument and reputable witnesses could be effective defenses against charges of magic. His defense thus gives insight into how the accused might protect themselves from these kinds of charges, which were often difficult to prove or disprove. In essence, *The Apology* offers a rich depiction of how magic was perceived and prosecuted in ancient Rome, while also illustrating the strategies an educated person could use to defend against such accusations. It highlights the fragile lines between magic, religion, and science in the Roman mindset, and how rhetoric and public opinion could influence legal outcomes in cases involving accusations of sorcery.

In *The Apology*, Apuleius reveals much about the nature of philosophical pursuits in the ancient world, particularly how they were often intertwined with mystical and scientific interests. Apuleius himself was deeply engaged in a range of studies that included natural science, Platonic philosophy, and religious rituals. However, his accusers misinterpreted or intentionally distorted these interests as evidence of magic, reflecting how philosophical and scientific knowledge was often viewed with suspicion. ### Key Philosophical Pursuits Highlighted in *The Apology*: 1. Platonic Philosophy: Apuleius was a Platonist, and he often invoked the name of Plato as a guiding figure for his intellectual and ethical pursuits. He mentions practices like contemplating the divine and studying natural phenomena, both of which were central to Platonic thought. His reference to Plato's teachings on the soul, the nature of the divine, and the pursuit of virtue underscores his commitment to philosophy as a path to understanding the world and achieving moral excellence. 2. Natural Science and Curiosity: Apuleius’s interest in natural science, such as his observations of fish and other natural phenomena, illustrates the inquisitive spirit of philosophers in antiquity. These pursuits were part of a broader intellectual endeavor to understand the workings of nature, which was sometimes perceived as bordering on the mystical. Apuleius makes it clear that his studies were scientific and philosophical rather than magical, reflecting a broader tension in the ancient world between curiosity-driven science and the fear of the unknown. 3. Theurgy and Religious Practices: Apuleius mentions certain religious rituals and practices, such as dedicating wood in a manner that his accusers construed as magical. In the Platonic tradition, theurgy (ritual practices intended to invoke the divine) was a means of engaging with the divine on a more personal level. Apuleius’s connection to these practices shows the intersection between philosophy and spirituality in his life, where intellectual inquiry could extend into spiritual and even mystical realms. 4. Defense of Intellectual Freedom: Throughout *The Apology*, Apuleius defends not only his own actions but also the broader right of philosophers to explore and question. His argument underscores the importance of intellectual freedom in philosophical pursuits, challenging the notion that curiosity and unconventional knowledge should be grounds for suspicion. This suggests that philosophical inquiry, especially when it involved the mysteries of nature or the divine, could be a risky endeavor in a society that feared the influence of unseen forces. 5. Rhetorical Skill and Logic: Apuleius’s defense also reflects his mastery of rhetoric, a skill that was highly valued among philosophers in ancient Rome. His arguments are structured logically, and he uses rhetorical techniques to persuade, disarm, and entertain his audience. This rhetorical prowess not only helped him in his defense but also highlighted the philosopher’s role as a communicator and defender of reason against superstition and false accusations. Overall, Apuleius’s philosophical pursuits demonstrate a blend of intellectual curiosity, scientific inquiry, and spiritual engagement, which was not uncommon among ancient philosophers. His defense reflects the way these pursuits could easily be misinterpreted as magic by those who didn’t understand them, illustrating the challenges philosophers faced in maintaining their reputations and intellectual freedoms in a world often wary of esoteric knowledge.

unpack theurgy and religious practices. then cite me chapter and verse on precursors in Plato and other philosophers Apuleius might have read or cites
ChatGPT
In *The Apology*, Apuleius’s references to religious practices and theurgy reflect an aspect of ancient philosophy that sought to engage with the divine not just through contemplation but also through ritual actions. Theurgy, as it developed in the later Platonic tradition, was a practice intended to invoke the presence or influence of divine beings, often through rituals or sacred acts. This engagement with the divine could bridge the gap between human and godly realms, aligning the philosopher more closely with the divine order. ### Theurgy in Apuleius and Its Philosophical Context Apuleius does not explicitly mention "theurgy" by name, as this term became more prominent in the works of later Neoplatonists like Iamblichus. However, his practices, such as consecrating wood or reciting particular prayers, suggest a form of theurgic activity—rituals meant to honor or draw closer to divine beings. This aligns with Platonic philosophy, which emphasized the philosopher's role in seeking divine truth and aligning oneself with higher realities beyond the material world. ### Platonic and Precursor Philosophical Sources on Theurgy 1. Plato: - Plato's *Phaedrus* (244a-257b): Plato discusses the role of divine madness (μανία) and inspiration in reaching higher truths. Although not strictly theurgic, this passage highlights the idea that humans can achieve a form of divine knowledge or insight that surpasses ordinary human understanding. The divine madness includes prophecy, love, poetry, and initiation into religious mysteries—all of which border on the practice of theurgy as a means to connect with the divine. - Plato's *Symposium* (202e-203a): Here, Diotima describes the *ladder of love*, where one ascends from physical beauty to the beauty of the soul, and ultimately to the divine. This ascent is a philosophical precursor to the idea of theurgy, as it involves spiritual progression toward the divine. 2. Porphyry: - *Letter to Anebo*: Although slightly later than Apuleius, Porphyry (a student of Plotinus) discusses practices that bridge philosophy and theurgy. He asks his teacher Anebo about the role of rituals and invocations, questioning how divine beings interact with humanity. Porphyry was critical of the more ritualistic elements, yet his writings show the tension between pure philosophy and theurgy. 3. Iamblichus: - *De Mysteriis* (On the Mysteries): Iamblichus, a follower of both Porphyry and Plato, defends theurgy as a way to unite the soul with the divine through sacred acts and rituals. He argues that intellectual contemplation alone is insufficient for such union; one must engage in divinely inspired rituals. Iamblichus expands on the Platonic theme of the soul’s ascent, incorporating theurgic practices as essential to achieving divine favor and understanding. 4. Apuleius’s Use of Platonic Precedents: - Apuleius seems to align with the Platonic tradition in treating the relationship with the divine as a process that transcends mere intellectual activity. By engaging in rituals, he is not simply studying the divine but invoking and honoring it, a practice that is philosophically grounded in Plato’s dialogues. Apuleius’s appeals to Plato (e.g., *The Apology* 22, where he invokes Socrates’ connection to divine signs or the daimonion) show that he likely saw his own practices as an extension of Platonic philosophy. 5. Neo-Pythagorean Influence: - The Neo-Pythagoreans, who combined Pythagorean mysticism with Platonic thought, influenced Apuleius and the development of theurgy. They often engaged in rituals that were intended to purify the soul and align it with the cosmic order, akin to the aims of theurgy. Apuleius would have encountered these ideas through the works of Plutarch and others who blended these traditions. Apuleius's religious practices illustrate how ancient philosophers often approached the divine as an experiential reality, accessible through both intellectual understanding and ritual action. His defense in *The Apology* suggests he viewed his rituals as aligned with a philosophical life, following precedents set by figures in the Platonic tradition and contemporaneous practices that influenced Neoplatonism.
